11/12/2007 10:29:13 PM

-------------------------------------------
Noble Intonation Approximations in Zest-24
An mp3 sampler of metastable interval zones
-------------------------------------------

Hello, everyone.

Recently David Keenan and others have taken part in discussions about
the "metastable" or "Nobly Intoned" (NI) intervals which the two of us
described in a paper of 2000:

<http://users.bigpond.net.au/d.keenan/Music/NobleMediant.txt>

Here I would like to share some mp3 files of cadential progressions
involving reasonably close approximations of many of these NI sizes as
found in the 24-note Zarlino Encompassing Spectrum Tuning, or Zest-24.
In short, an interval of around NI size may represent the zone of
maximum harmonic complexity between two simpler ratios. For example,
the 5:4 major third at 386 cents and 9:7 major third at 435 cents may
have an intervening region of "gravitational equipoise," or maximum
complexity, at around 422 cents.

The paper explains David Keenan's ingenious process for estimating
this NI value by using a Phi-weighted mediant of the two simpler
ratios. Here, however, the purpose is not so much to analyze this
process as to hear the audible results.

Zest-24 is called an "Encompassing Spectrum Tuning" because it surveys
many different regions and subregions of interval space. In my notes
for the mp3 examples which follow, I describe these regions in terms
which hopefully will be reasonably self-explanatory, but are more
fully developed in my newly completed paper exploring one possible
approach to the interval spectrum.

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/IntervalSpectrumRegions.txt>

As mentioned at the beginning of this paper, I am much indebted to the
very thoughtful and musically sensitive writings of David Keenan on
interval names and regions and much else. Our counterpoint of ideas,
like a musical counterpoint, seems to proceed now by similar and now
by contrary motion.

<http://users.bigpond.net.au/d.keenan/Music/>

The Zest-24 system uses two 12-note circles of a modified meantone
tuning based on Gioseffo Zarlino's 2/7-comma meantone. Each circle has
eight regular meantone fifths (F-C#), with the other four equally
wide; the two circles are at the distance of Zarlino's meantone
diesis, 50.276 cents. A Scala file is included at the end of this
article.

The name for each mp3 file starts with "NIA" for "Noble Intonation
Approximation," and includes the sizes in rounded cents for the
relevant NI approximations used in a given example. Here I have
specified the sizes which theoretically should occur in my specific
synthesizer realization of Zest-24 in 1024-EDO, which may sometimes
vary by something on the order of a cent from the values appearing in
the Scala file below.

----------
EXAMPLE 1.
----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA001-422.926.mp3>

This example includes two simple and very characteristic neomedieval
cadences using a 422-cent major third and 926-cent major sixth, the
first virtually coinciding with the estimated NI zone at 422 cents or
so, and the second slightly wide of this zone at around 923 cents.
The sonority Ab-C-F (or G#-C-F in a typical meantone spelling) found
in either 12-note circle includes these intervals. The resolution has
the major third expand to a fifth and the major sixth to an octave.

----------
EXAMPLE 2.
---------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA002-337.829.mp3>

This example features the small neutral third and sixth at 337 and 829
cents, not too far from NI values of 339 and 833 cents -- these
intervals, in either shading, may also be called supraminor thirds and
sixths. Here the third expands to the fifth, and the sixth to the
octave. A helpful spelling might be C-Eb*-Ab*, with the asterisk (*)
showing a note on the upper keyboard raised by a 50-cent diesis.

----------
EXAMPLE 3.
----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA003-337.mp3>

This example shows the use of the 337-cent small neutral third in a
progression where it contracts to a unison; the unstable sonority is
C-Eb*-G.

----------
EXAMPLE 4.
----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA004-791.1500.mp3>

An interesting feature of the theory of metastable or Nobly Intoned
intervals is that sometimes these zones can involve quite familiar
sizes, here a 791-cent minor sixth almost identical with the
Pythagorean 128:81 or rounded NI value (both 792 cents), and a minor
tenth at an even 1500 cents which approximates the NI value of 1503
cents. These intervals (G-Eb-Bb in either circle) expand to the octave
and 12th.

----------
EXAMPLE 5.
----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA005-287.422.mp3>

This standard neomedieval cadence uses a minor third at 287 cents,
slightly larger than the NI value of around 284 cents, with the NI
major third at around 422 cents between the middle and upper voices
(0-422-708 cents), a tuning found at Eb-Gb-Bb in either circle.
Here minor third contracts to unison and major third expands to
fifth.

----------
EXAMPLE 6.
----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA006a-287.422.longer.mp3>

This example has the same final cadence as the last, but approached
from the diminished seventh sonority D-F-Ab-Cb (0-313-587-888 cents)
available in either circle (compare 15:18:21:25, 0-316-583-884 cents).
The diminished seventh contracts to the outer fifth of a sonority with
a 4-3 suspension, the fourth resolving to a _minor_ third that then
resolves to a unison, as in the previous example.

----------
EXAMPLE 7.
----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA007-791.mp3>

This cadence uses the sonority G-Eb-B with its two almost identical
intervals of 791 cents (G-Eb) and 792 cents (Eb-B) in a kind of
Manneristic meantone idiom treating Eb-B as an augmented fifth.
The very slight inequality of these two intervals is a quirk of the
synthesizer temperament.

----------
EXAMPLE 8.
----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA008-946.mp3>

This neomedieval cadence features what I term in my new paper an
_interseptimal_ interval of around 946 cents, located in the
fascinated region between the septimal ratios of 12:7 (933 cents) for
a large major sixth and 7:4 (969 cents) for a small minor seventh.
Here it is coupled with another dynamic interseptimal interval at
around 454 cents, virtually identical to a just 13:10, in the region
between the septimal 9:7 major third at 435 cents and the narrow 21:16
fourth at 471 cents. In this context, these intervals (C*-F-Bb) serve
as an "ultramajor" third and sixth expanding to fifth and octave with
the upper voices rising by 50-cent steps.

The 454-cent interval showcased in this example, by the way, is almost
identical to Gene Ward Smith's "ratwolf" equal to precisely to 13:10,
also his name for a regular meantone temperament differing minutely
from 2/7-comma which produces a "RAT(ional) WOLF" at this just ratio
from a chain of 11 fifths up. In Zest-24, the 2/7-comma "near-ratwolf"
is tempered out or "domesticated" within each 12-note circle, but
happily "reintroduced into the wild" by the 50-cent diesis between the
two circles.

----------
EXAMPLE 9.
----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA009-926.mp3>

This Manneristic cadence in a meantone style uses a 926-cent major
sixth (or here diminished seventh) in the sonority C#-E-G-Bb at around
0-313-626-926 cents, followed by a 4-3 suspension and resolution.

-----------
EXAMPLE 10.
-----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA010-422.2113.mp3>

An interesting feature of metastable or NI intervals underscored in a
recent list contributed by David Keenan is that octave extensions can
make a difference. Here the metastable or NI major third at 422 cents
is coupled with a near-NI major 13th at 2113 cents, close to the
estimated NI zone at around 2109 cents. These intervals (Gb3-Bb3-Eb5
with C4 as middle C), available within either circle, resolve to fifth
and 15th (or double octave) respectively.

-----------
EXAMPLE 11.
-----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA011-2055.mp3>

Another example of an NI interval larger than octave, this cadence has
a 2055-cent neutral 13th, here used together with a 2752-cent neutral
third plus two octaves or neutral 17th (Ab2-E*4-B*4). These intervals
expand to the 15th or double octave and 19th or fifth plus double
octave. This wide a spacing of voices is rather rare in a neomedieval
style; but this example and the last show how certain NI intervals
well beyond the octave may provide an incentive.

-----------
EXAMPLE 12.
-----------

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/NIA012-422.mp3>

This concluding example shows how the 422-cent NI major third may be
used as an idiomatic meantone diminished fourth in a Manneristic
style. Here we start at D-G-Bb-D, with the fourth and minor sixth
above the bass suggesting a cadential approach. The fourth G then
proceeds to the major third F# -- forming the diminished fourth F#-Bb
at 422 cents, which leads to a 4-3 suspension and resolution.

----------

As promised, here is the Zest-24 tuning:

! zest24.scl
!
Zarlino Encompassing Spectrum Temperament (two circles at ~50.28c apart)
24
!

Most appreciatively,

Margo Schulter

> > click on "reply" in order to see the chart properly, due to this
> > website changing the displayed format:

11/13/2007 9:39:55 AM

Hi Tony,

We have discussed this before on the List. My players are able to perform
best in cents notation, if only because it encompasses all manner of different
tunings. There really is little difference between polymicrotonal tuning
and 1200-tone equal temperament as I interpret it. For real time, seat of the
pants playing, cents notation, with only two new symbols (quarterflat and
quartersharp), deviations of 1-49 above or below any note are easily negotiated.
New memorization of different symbols is perhaps better suite for
electronic/keyboard based instruments, instruments with preset tuning as opposed to
flexible tuning.

Johnny

11/13/2007 11:54:18 AM

--- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, "J.A.Martin Salinas" <tony@...> wrote:
>
> I have just read this from the Sagittal notation doc.:
>
> We have also notated even higher divisions such as 270, 282, 306,
> 311, 342, 388, 494, 612, and beyond, as well as many below 224
>
> I would love to see if you guys have done any progress in these
> higher divisions like 612
>
> It sounds a very exciting work.

Yep, as it says there, we are indeed able to notate 612-ET with
Sagittal (as well as many beyond that, up to 2460-ET). In fact,
there's more than one way to do 612, but we haven't yet decided which
should be the standard notation, which is why we haven't released
anything specific. (Much the same could be said for the other
divisions on that list.)

Your question is very timely, because Dave Keenan and I have just
finished defining all of the symbols that will be used for just
intonation (which, in turn, will help us to determine how the above
ET's should be notated). We expect to be releasing documentation
very shortly regarding this, including a spreadsheet that will show
how to notate (with alternate spellings) any ratio in Sagittal
through prime limit 61 (using prime factor exponents, or "monzos").

> I would also love to hear Johnny's opinion about these symbols if
> they get as accurate as his 1200edo notation.

Since it's able to notate 2460-EDO, Sagittal is capable of roughly
twice the precision, though it's debatable whether that's really
necessary. For those don't want to cope with the many accidentals
this requires, we offer lower-precision JI options: e.g., extreme-
precision (olympian-level) JI corresponds to ~2460-EDO, whereas high-
precision (herculean-level and promethean-level) JI is comparable to
612-EDO and 494-EDO (respectively), and medium-precision (athenian-
level) JI to 224-EDO. If that's still too many accentals for you,
then we would recommend mapping your tones to either 130-ET (using
the spartan symbol set) or 72-ET (which uses less than half of the
spartan set).

> Do you think Johnny Reinhard, that this could improve the sight-
> reading of your musicians if you had a chance
> to teach them from scratch???

Johnny's notation is well suited to enabling players to arrive at the
desired pitches on conventional flexible-pitch instruments. Sagittal
is best suited to providing a meaningful notation for retuned fixed-
pitch instruments, for specially built microtonal wind instruments,
for the electronic medium, and for theoretical applications. The two
approaches are quite different and are, in fact, complementary: each
tends to excel in applications where the other is weakest.

--George

Hi Tony, Johnny, and George,

--- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, "George D. Secor" <gdsecor@...> wrote:

> > I would also love to hear Johnny's opinion about
> > these symbols if they get as accurate as his 1200edo
> > notation.

> Since it's able to notate 2460-EDO, Sagittal is capable
> of roughly twice the precision, though it's debatable
> whether that's really necessary.

Hmm ... first reading Johnny's answer to Tony, in which
he describes his 1200-edo notation as employing all the
regular accidentals, with additional quarterflat and
quartersharp symbols, and 0-49 cent markings to indicate
deviations from those ... and then George's description
here of 2460-edo (minas), got me to thinking ...

It's too bad that 2460 doesn't divide evenly into 24.
The regular-plus-quartertone accidental set and deviation
numbers 0-100 would make a nice reference tuning for
the notation.

Actually, Johnny, you don't even need the quatertone
symbols for 1200-edo because you can just use the
regular sharps and flats and +/-50 cents and still
get precision to 1200-edo, if you can do without the
enharmonic spellings of notes which would need the
quartertone symbols.

2460-edo could theoretically be acheived in a similar way
by using the regular sharps and flats plus numerical
indications +/-102.5 degrees, so you can still get
close enough for practical use by using +/-100. If
the lowernote-plus-100 is not equal to uppernote-minus-100,
so that there is one degree between those two values,
then you get 201 degrees per 12-edo semitone, which
is 2412-edo.

You could also employ quartertone symbols to fit between
these two values, without using any numerical deviations
when using the quartertone symbols, in which case you
get 202 degrees per semitone = 2424-edo.

Anyway, just some ideas i had. The system Johnny already
has in use feels very natural to a trained microtonal
musician, because the quarter-tones are "anchors" at
the 50-cent points, and so using +/-50 cents (without
the quartertone accidentals) is an easy way for a performer
to develop ear training to acheive the desired results.

> For those don't want to cope with the many accidentals
> this requires, we offer lower-precision JI options:
> e.g., extreme-precision (olympian-level) JI corresponds
> to ~2460-EDO, whereas high-precision (herculean-level
> and promethean-level) JI is comparable to 612-EDO and
> 494-EDO (respectively), and medium-precision (athenian-
> level) JI to 224-EDO. If that's still too many accentals
> for you, then we would recommend mapping your tones
> to either 130-ET (using the spartan symbol set) or 72-ET
> (which uses less than half of the spartan set).

Myself, i still like 72-edo HEWM. ;-P

I'm still looking forward to having HEWM and Sagittal
notations in Tonescape someday, so i can compare the
same piece notated both ways.

11/13/2007 3:51:28 PM

Hi Joe, Tony, George,

Joe: Actually, Johnny, you don't even need the quatertone
symbols for 1200-edo because you can just use the
regular sharps and flats and +/-50 cents and still
get precision to 1200-edo, if you can do without the
enharmonic spellings of notes which would need the
quartertone symbols.

Johnny: Not ready to dump the tonal implications, some only psychological,
of sharps to flats, that a more pure number approach would seem to be an
improvement. The quartertone sharp and the quartertone flat are visual landmarks
(not audible landmarks). They allow the conservatory trained music reader to
follow a logic they come to expect as normal to playing. In addition,
numbers 1-49 are clearly more likely to be numbers less than 25. 50 breaks the
bank. Numbers larger than 50 are hard to negotiate on the hoof.

I agree with George.

Joe: Anyway, just some ideas i had. The system Johnny already
has in use feels very natural to a trained microtonal
musician, because the quarter-tones are "anchors" at
the 50-cent points,

Johnny: I think they are much more visual anchors for sight reading skill
expectations more than they are soundposts for hearing an interval.

Joe: and so using +/-50 cents (without
the quartertone accidentals) is an easy way for a performer
to develop ear training to acheive the desired results.

Johnny: Sorry to pick (or poke), but where is the ear training from the
following of visual symbology? They are apples and oranges here. The hearing
of, and the initiating of, discrete microtonal intervals is independent of
actually playing it. In fast movement the eye goes to a quartertone specific
sector (quadrant?) and the deviation of a norm is quickly calculated. Even
with this, I use a notation shorthand for quick fingerings, but I still need the
number of cents to set the intellect of my mind. Both are necessary.

But there is also a difference between genuine sight reading, and
preparation preceding group reading. At a certain point, a player employs other
symbology; I use a pair of glasses to make me alert at a certain point in a piece
of music. I circle alternative fingerings to distinguish them from the much
more usual fingerings(generally yielding monophonic pivots, if not simply
alternative timbres). There are some fingerings for ease of motion, for
trilling, for dynamic, for timbre, for pitch sensitivity (one with open tone holes
and one with the gradual depression of a key). Of course, I know you know much
of this.

Joe: Myself, i still like 72-edo HEWM. ;-P
I'm still looking forward to having HEWM and Sagittal
notations in Tonescape someday, so i can compare the
same piece notated both ways.
-monz

Johnny: Great art is made out of the material found about it. There are
many ways to segregate. 72 does not yield just intervals that I miss. But I
still think it is the composer that makes the difference, not the tuning.
(But it does help some individuals over the hump of being derivative, the method
that is so abundant taught in contemporary music education.)

11/13/2007 8:50:16 PM

> Here are the 4 sets (transpositions of the scale), layed out
> differently, in case my chart was confusing people! (Margo I hope you
> can read it better now).

> 1st set
> D, D#-25, F, G, A, Bb-25, C
> 2nd set
> G, Ab-25, Bb, C, D, D#-25, F
> 3rd set
> A, Bb-25, C, D, E, F-25, G
> 4th set
> C, C#-25, D#, F, G, Ab-25, Bb

Dear Justin,

Thank you so much! Arigatogozaimas! Now I can read your chart without
any problem

Also, please let me thank you very warmly for the _Haru-no-Kyoku_ mp3,
which sounds to me very often polyphonic, and sometimes seems to use
what is called in Europe "imitation" -- that is, having the same
melodic theme or pattern sounded first in one voice, and then in
another.

Your new listing of the four sets is very helpful in explaining a
possible reason for the tuning I have proposed. However, please let me
emphasize that _it is not necessarily better than your tuning above,
or Herman's_. Either of those might be better, and also closer to the
prevailing practice as you have described it!

Herman, please feel warmly welcome to step in at any point. I suspect
that we are illustrating two approaches to tuning, and I hope that I
am correctly interpretating your intentions. As I emphasize below,
your tunings of minor thirds and sevenths are just as good as mine,
and my idea is to experiment with the idea of a single chain of nine
fifths to express the four transposition sets -- six pure fifths and
three tempered by a tad less than five cents.

A possible advantage of my tuning is that it could be done on certain
instruments with fewer than 13 notes available per octave, since it
would allow us to use a single string, flute hole, or key on a
keyboard to represent what have traditionally been two separate
pitches. Thus we have in the usual style C# and C#-25, D# and D#-25,
and F and F-25.

Here I would prefer to spell C# as Db, since C to Db-25 in the 4th set
is a usual semitone, and Db-25 to Ab-25 is a regular fifth. However, I
understand that Western note spellings may sometimes be confusing, and
might actually myself be quite happy simply to use cents.

Why don't I first show my ten-note tuning in which we would need only
one version rather than two for Db, F, and Ab; and then the four sets
as they would be played in this tuning. Here I would add the Western
note names at the end of each line, not being sure if they are
helpful, and also column listing of where the note appears in each
set, with an "n" meaning it is not used in a given set. For example,
the notation "D:154n" tells us that D is the first step of the 1st
set, the fifth step of the 2nd, the fourth step of the 3rd, and is not
used in the 4th. I've tried to get the four columns to line up.

0: 1/1 0.000 unison, perfect prime (D:;154n)
1: 22/21 80.537 undecimal minor semitone (Eb:26n3)
2: 9/8 203.910 major whole tone (E:;nn5n)
3: 33/28 284.447 undecimal minor third (F:;3764)
4: 4/3 498.045 perfect fourth (G::4175)
5: 88/63 578.582 (Ab:n2n6)
6: 3/2 701.955 perfect fifth (A::5n1n)
7: 11/7 782.492 undecimal minor sixth (Bb:6327)
8: 39/22 991.165 tridecimal minor seventh (C:;7431)
9: 13/7 1071.702 16/3-tone (Db:nnn2)
10: 2/1 1200.000 octave (D:;154n)

We may need to discuss some concepts a bit, if you are curious,
because I realize that the theory can get rather complicated. However,
the basic idea is that if we'd like a note to be in two different
places in different sets, maybe we can put it somewhere between those
two places, and then leave it where it is for both sets. This is a
compromise, and if you find that it's better just to move that note as
convenient, then you would not be wrong!

Maybe the best way to start explaining is to see how each of the four
sets will be tuned with this scale of only ten notes in the octave.
Here I will keep the original numbers of the ten notes for each set.

1st set

0: 1/1 0.000 unison, perfect prime (D:;154n)
1: 22/21 80.537 undecimal minor semitone (Eb:26n3)
3: 33/28 284.447 undecimal minor third (F:;3764)
4: 4/3 498.045 perfect fourth (G::4175)
6: 3/2 701.955 perfect fifth (A::5n1n)
7: 11/7 782.492 undecimal minor sixth (Bb:6327)
8: 39/22 991.165 tridecimal minor seventh (C:;7431)
10: 2/1 1200.000 octave (D:;154n)

2nd set

4: 1/1 0.000 unison, perfect prime (G)
5: 22/21 80.537 undecimal minor semitone (Ab)
7: 33/28 284.447 undecimal minor third (Bb)
8: 117/88 493.120 fourth narrow by 4.925 cents (C)
10: 3/2 701.955 perfect fifth (D)
1: 11/7 782.492 undecimal minor sixth (Eb)
3: 99/56 986.402 undecimal minor seventh (F)
4: 2/1 1200.000 octave (G)

3rd set

6: 1/1 0.000 unison, perfect prime (A)
7: 22/21 80.537 undecimal minor semitone (Bb)
8: 13/11 289.210 tridecimal minor third (C)
0: 4/3 498.045 perfect fourth (D)
2: 3/2 701.955 perfect fifth (E)
3: 11/7 782.492 undecimal minor sixth (F)
4: 16/9 996.090 Pythagorean minor seventh (G)
6: 2/1 1200.000 octave (A)

4th set

8: 1/1 0.000 unison, perfect prime (C)
9: 22/21 80.537 undecimal minor semitone (Db)
1: 968/819 289.372 (Eb)
3: 121/91 493.282 (F)
4: 176/117 706.880 (G)
5: 3872/2457 787.417 (Ab)
7: 484/273 991.327 (Bb)
8: 2/1 1200.000 octave (C)

The secret is that we are using only a single chain of fifths for the
whole tuning, nine fifths in all to give our ten notes per octave. Six
of these fifths are pure, as in Herman's tuning also, where three of
them are made wide by about five cents each.

Note that the tunings vary a little from set to set, but that they
should all be reasonably tolerable, if my guess is correct -- this is
for you to judge!

Indeed many of the intervals will be identical to those of Herman's
fine tuning. Thus note that the 1st and 3rd sets have a pure fifth and
a pure fourth above the first note (D or A), and that in the 3rd set,
from A to G is a Pythagorean minor seventh at 16:9 or 996 cents.

At the same time, melodically, you will see that the semitone steps in
each of these four sets, found between the first and second steps of a
given set and also the fifth and sixth steps, are about 81 cents,
somewhat wider than the preferred 75 cents, but yet smaller than the
90 cents we would get tuning with all fifths and fourths pure.

This brings me to the idea of a "chain of fifths," which you also
mentioned in a message with questions for Herman. I will try and show
the chain of fifths in this tuning, starting with Db and going to E.

Db--+4.9--Ab--0--Eb--0--Bb--0--F--+4.8--C--+4.9--G--0--D--0--A--0--E

Here a "0" between two numbers means a pure fifth, and "+4.9" or
"+4.8" shows that a fifth is tempered wide by about 4.9 or 4.8 cents.

> Could you tell me why you prefer "33/28 284.447 undecimal minor
> third" to Herman's suggestion "32/27 294.135 Pythagorean minor
> third"?

Actually I think that these are both fine minor thirds, and very much
like both flavors or shadings, as they might be called. The possible
advantage of 33:28 is that, as one pleasing kind of minor third, it
might be closer to a pure fourth in relation to your preferred size of
minor sixth around 775 cents -- or, here, often 782 or 787 cents.
Actually, 284 is closer to a pure fourth below 782 cents, while either
284 or 294 cents (32:27) is comparably close to a pure fourth below
787 cents.

> And "39/22 991.165 tridecimal minor seventh" to Herman's suggestion
> "16/9 996.090 Pythagorean minor seventh"?

Either of these minor sevenths is fine! I'd be happy to use either. In
fact, if you are playing polyphony and play a note, its fourth, and
minor seventh all together -- about 0-498-996 cents -- then you might
prefer the pure 16:9 minor seventh of Herman's tuning, because both
fourths will be pure. With my version, if you play D-G-C in the first
set at the same time, D-G will be a pure fourth, but G-C will be
almost five cents narrow. I use this kind of sound often, but you
would not be wrong to prefer pure intervals.

What I may need to explain in a more careful and step-by-step way is
how a chain of fifths, or more than one chain, can be used to built a
tuning. The idea of my tuning is to have as many reasonably useful
fifths and possible with the smallest number of notes per octave; have
most of these fifths and fourths pure; and have scale steps like the
second and sixth in a given set fairly close to their usual sizes as
you have described them.

Most appreciatively,

Margo
mschulter@calweb.com

11/19/2007 3:10:48 PM

> Inspired by Margo, I came up with a splendid maqam tuning with at least one
> nobly intoned ratio.

> First, calculate the noble mediant between 27:25 and 14:13.
> (27+14)*phi / (25+13)*phi=131.55 cents

> In SCALA, type:
> equal 31
> copy 0 1
> move
> 131.55
> merge
> 1
> Voila!
> Oz.

Dear Oz and Dave,

Please let me diplomatically attempt here to mediate in a situation
which I suspect must occur many times in the realm of musical practice
and theory.

Oz, you have used a mathematical formula which Dave devised and he and
I documented for rather different purposes -- but to excellent effect,
deriving a fine size of small neutral second for maqam music. The step
of 131.55 cents should be especially welcome to Persian or Kurdish
ears, for example, attuned to maqam or dastgah styles where a step on
the order of 14:13 is very idiomatic. Here the mathematical noble
mediant between 14:13 and 27:25 -- or also 1:1 and 13:12, as Dave has
suggested -- happens to yield a fine melodic effect. I see no reason
not to use this mathematical formula, or any mathematical formula,
when it produces a result that fits the musical situation.

Dave, you are equally correct that the concept of Noble Intonation
was, if I am correct, conceived as especially applying to
_simultaneous_ intervals which represent regions of maximum complexity
between two simpler ratios. A classic illustration, of course, is the
pair of major thirds at a just 5:4 and 9:7, with the NI region located
somewhere around 422 cents.

Thus I warmly agree that it would be a misunderstanding of the
original NI concept to think that _any_ mathematical noble mediant
between a pair of integer ratios will produce an audible "NI" effect.
In fact, Oz's purpose seems to me quite different: to strike a kind of
artful compromise between two sizes for _melodic_ steps, 14:13 and
27:25. The mathematical noble mediant operation happens to produce a
most agreeable result -- although that wasn't its original purpose.

Dave, I would say that one of the hallmarks of really great concepts
and discoveries is that they may be used in various ways. Sometimes
applications not anticipated by the discoverer may arise -- for better
or worse -- and even be (mis)attributed to the discoverer.

Here I would say, "Oz -- congratulations on a fine maqam tuning!"; and
to Dave, "Indeed, while the formula was used here to pleasant effect,
people should understand that the NI concept which gave rise to the
formula is somewhat different."

With warmest wishes to you both, and all,

Margo Schulter
mschulter@calweb.com

Margo Schulter <mschulter@calweb.com>

11/22/2007 11:28:50 AM

[Brad]

> And to complete the picture of these several concerts, the rest of
> our first half (the 17th century half) was an Uccellini sonata in A
> minor, Venice 1649, which happened to have some B major triads in it
> (cadencing into E minor, repeatedly), and an F# major triad. And
> the other accompanied piece was a Schmelzer sonata in G minor from
> 1664, which happens to need the note A-flat. [And then he played
> the Biber unaccompanied Passacaglia, and then we went on to the
> Corelli as I mentioned above.]

Hi, Brad. Indeed this is a very interesting period, for me more or
less _ultra_-modern, and in one style of periodization we would say
that the 1640's might mark the end of the Manneristic era (1540-1640
or so) and the beginning of the Early Baroque. In another traditional
approach, the Baroque starts around 1600 with the rise of continuo
notation, opera, and oratorio, so the whole 17th century would be
included.

One small point, where I know that usages differ, although maybe not
so relevantly for the intonational questions we're considering: While
it's common to apply major/minor key labels to early 17th-century
music, I might prefer to follow the composer's designation, whatever
it is, for example a given tone or mode. Of course, if composers are
already using major/minor terminology, then that would be both
appropriate and very noteworthy -- for Uccellini, I'm not sure.

> Again: what would you have us do? 1/4 comma meantone doesn't really
> work for *any* of these pieces, unless we're supposed to put up with
> ridiculous-sounding B major and F# major in normal cadencing
> situations.

Actually my guess is that 1/4-comma meantone should be just fine, if
you use a tuning set which has accidentals with proper spellings for
sonorities like B-D#-F# and F#-A#-C#. This would often involve the
very authentic period choice of an instrument with more than 12 notes
per octave.

While an instrument with only a couple of split keys (G#/Ab, Eb/D#)
would address a lot of common situations, here we also need A#, which
might suggest an instrument with at least 17 notes per octave. A usual
_cembalo cromatico_ of the kind popular around Naples in the epoch of
Gesualdo, with 19 notes per octave likely tuned in 1/4-comma, would
neatly address the situation.

This is the kind of 19-note keyboard recommended by Praetorius, and
also taken by Fabio Colonna (1618) as the basis for tuning the first
19 notes of his 31 note _Sambuca Lincea_ evidently based on a cycle of
1/4-comma meantone closely approximating 31-EDO. Of course, Colonna is
championing a 31-note meantone cycle which goes back to Nicola
Vicentino (1555, 1561).

In 1666, Lemme Rossi discusses both extended 1/4-comma meantone and a
precise 31-EDO, with Christiaan Huygens in 1691 advocating "The New
Harmonic Cycle" based on 31-EDO, which he also shows differs only
minutely from the familiar 1/4-comma.

Of course, one could construct examples where extended 1/4-comma (or
31-EDO) would run into problems: specifically, if a composer relies on
enharmonic equivalence, as Vincenzo Galilei does in the late 16th
century specifically to show the "perfection" of 12-EDO and the lute
as an instrument customarily using it.

However, unless this kind of equivalence is demanded, my guess is that
the problem you are describing is not inherent to 1/4-comma, but
rather to instruments which don't cover a large enough range of it to
have available the needed accidentals for a given piece.

I realize, of course, that this is a rather obvious point; but since
period practices are such an important consideration in this thread,
maybe mentioning split-key accidentals isn't out of place.

This leaves the question, "What should we do if only a 12-note
keyboard is available?" That's an intriguing question. Of course, one
period solution (however controversial) is a regular tuning at or not
too far from 12-EDO, which Mersenne discusses in the 1630's, and Denis
heartily disapproves in his treatise on keyboard tuning and music
(1643, 1650). Doni tells the story that Frescobaldi recommended 12-EDO
for a new church organ which Doni persuaded those responsible to tune
in the usual meantone -- however accurate or otherwise this tale.

Another is some kind of temperament ordinaire based on something
around 1/4-comma. An interesting question is what context F#-A#-C#
appears in. If it's used as a cadential sonority leading to B, then a
bit of ornamentation might help (a la Schlick 1511). Mark Lindley
suggests that even an unaltered 1/4-comma diminished fourth at 32:25
(427 cents) might be used in ornamented contexts, although an
irregular scheme that ameliorates F#-A# to about Pythagorean could
further ease this dilemma.

Again, 1650 is really late for me, so I'd welcome comments from Tom or
others more conversant with this epoch.

Most appreciatively,

Margo Schulter
mschulter@calweb.com

Brad Lehman <bpl@umich.edu>

11/22/2007 1:30:50 PM

> Another is some kind of temperament ordinaire based on something
> around 1/4-comma. An interesting question is what context F#-A#-C#
> appears in. If it's used as a cadential sonority leading to B, then a
> bit of ornamentation might help (a la Schlick 1511). Mark Lindley
> suggests that even an unaltered 1/4-comma diminished fourth at 32:25
> (427 cents) might be used in ornamented contexts, although an
> irregular scheme that ameliorates F#-A# to about Pythagorean could
> further ease this dilemma.

In the Uccellini piece we played, it had me sitting on B major for a
whole bar at a time while the violin played melodic/ornamental stuff,
and then E minor for the whole next bar. This happened several times.
Sure, I could have made up more ornamental stuff of my own during
some of that, and I probably did, but there's also the danger of
competing too much with the melodic part. And if I would have had to
do it every time there's a whole bar that is sounding cruddy, just
because a bad temperament has been set up...well, that's just not a
very good musical reason for that much ornamentation (in my opinion).
The ornamentation itself would have had so many wrong enharmonics in
it (if forced to use a 1/4 comma system), it would just be a mess.

Similarly, as I mentioned, the Corelli D major sonata we played (from
1700) had a bunch of F# major and C# major in it. Some sections
modulated into G# minor, as well. And, in that sonata, the
harpsichord or organ had to play those harmonies for a whole bar (with
a somewhat moving bass line, too), in long sequences, while the violin
did bariolage figurations in continuous motion: four-note chords of
his own, broken up. Again, not a great place to try to make up
right-hand dodgy continuo stuff just because an F would be way too
high to serve as E#, or a Bb way too high to be A#.

The far more elegant solution is to scrap the unworkable notion of 1/4
comma around the naturals, open the thing up, and have some room to
breathe!

I don't see any compelling reason to borrow advice from Schlick 1511
to play music from more than 130 years later (the Uccellini and
Schmelzer), or 189 years (the Corelli)! :)

=====

To try out the new Yale University organ a few months ago (while it
was still inside the factory in Virginia), I brought along a book of
Froberger. The instrument has regular 1/4 comma everywhere, and
several split keys for the subsemitones (two or three splits in each
octave, except for the bass). Well...it still didn't work. I tried
out several pieces that were harmonically adventurous, from the
1640s-60s. And they just sounded stupid whenever notes such as E# and
B# turned up, having no split key available. No amount of
ornamentation salvaged such spots, especially in a piece by Froberger
that *ends* each of its sections on a C# major triad!

Nor do I buy the fantasy argument that organists simply transposed the
whole piece (if indeed they played composed pieces in public at
all...); one of the Froberger toccatas I played needed not only
E#/A#/D#/G#/C#/F#, but also Db/Ab/Eb/Bb and F and C. And it's
definitely an organ piece: by its title, it's for use during the
elevation of the host during a liturgy. There's no wholesale
transposition of the composition available that would get it to work
on extended regular 1/4 comma, even if all five raised keys got
split...because it would also have to have one or more split *naturals*.

The failure here is not the physical keyboard with its limitations,
but rather the premise of 1/4 comma for such repertoire. I find it
sad to see a brand-new, deluxe, expensive organ go into service, with
a temperament scheme unable to play early-to-mid-17th-century core
repertoire. (Some Frescobaldi would have problems, too.) But, they
built it the way the customer wanted and ordered it....

Brad Lehman

Margo Schulter <mschulter@calweb.com>

11/23/2007 11:34:37 AM

> In the Uccellini piece we played, it had me sitting on B major for a
> whole bar at a time while the violin played melodic/ornamental stuff,
> and then E minor for the whole next bar. This happened several times.

Hi, Brad.

Please let me agree that in this kind of situation, ornamentation
isn't such a good solution: assuming that D# and Eb both occur, I'd
say that a likely period solution would be either a split-key
instrument with both accidentals, or a continuo instrument like a lute
with approximately equal semitones where D# and Eb would be
equivalent.

The 17-note organ you describe below would solve this kind of
situation admirably, although, as described below, I would have urged
them to add E# and B# as well for a standard 19-note tuning (Gb-B#) of
the kind very popular in areas like Naples around 1600.

> Sure, I could have made up more ornamental stuff of my own during
> some of that, and I probably did, but there's also the danger of
> competing too much with the melodic part. And if I would have had
> to do it every time there's a whole bar that is sounding cruddy,
> just because a bad temperament has been set up...well, that's just
> not a very good musical reason for that much ornamentation (in my
> opinion).

Here by "bad temperament," I might read, "a temperament which, at
least with the number of notes available on this instrument, does not
very well fit the piece in question."

> The ornamentation itself would have had so many wrong enharmonics in
> it (if forced to use a 1/4 comma system), it would just be a mess.

Friendly amendment: "if forced to use a 1/4-comma system with an
insufficient accidental range to accommodate the piece." This isn't to
say, of course, that because one _could_ play Bach in a 31-note circle
of 1/4-comma or 31-EDO, therefore one _should_: we likely agree that a
12-note well-temperament is much more idiomatic. I'd say that your
interpretation of the 1722 diagram is one very interesting contender,
with some Neidhardt temperaments also very plausible. However, I
should caution that I feel on much more confident ground when
discussing modal rather than tonal styles.

> Similarly, as I mentioned, the Corelli D major sonata we played
> (from 1700) had a bunch of F# major and C# major in it. Some
> sections modulated into G# minor, as well. And, in that sonata, the
> harpsichord or organ had to play those harmonies for a whole bar
> (with a somewhat moving bass line, too), in long sequences, while
> the violin did bariolage figurations in continuous motion:
> four-note chords of his own, broken up. Again, not a great place to
> try to make up right-hand dodgy continuo stuff just because an F
> would be way too high to serve as E#, or a Bb way too high to be
> A#. The far more elegant solution is to scrap the unworkable
> notion of 1/4 comma around the naturals, open the thing up, and
> have some room to breathe!

Since Corelli's style is often regarded as the clear advent of
major/minor tonality, I'd say that there could be arguments for a
temperament less heavy that 1/4-comma on melodic grounds; we might
want a narrower diatonic semitone narrower than 117 cents, for example.
Around 1700, there's evidence that people often preferred something
around 1/5-1/6 comma, not to speak of various irregular schemes.

However, a strictly technical answer to your remarks is that a 19-note
tuning of 1/4-comma (Gb-B#), the kind of thing that had been known for
at least about 150 years by 1700, would provide both A# and E#, as
well as B# if that should come up. Again, I'm not saying that it's the
best temperament for repertory around 1700, only that from a view of
having the right accidentals, it allows considerable "room to breathe."

> I don't see any compelling reason to borrow advice from Schlick
> 1511 to play music from more than 130 years later (the Uccellini
> and Schmelzer), or 189 years (the Corelli)! :)

While it was my purpose to honor Schlick for his description of what
might be called the first known modified meantone tuning, I should
emphasize that for music around 1650, there are some later clues and
theorists that suggest similar conclusions. Thus Praetorius in 1618 or
so reports that some people tune 1/4-comma so as to compromise notes
such as G#/Ab -- much, I would add, as Schlick describes in 1511.

Also, Werckmeister in his _Orgel Probe_ of 1698 urges people who want
to stick with the "Praetorian temperament" (i.e. 1/4-comma) to
consider at least doing precisely this kind of thing with Eb/D#, for
example. I'm not recommending it as an ideal solution, but mentioning
it as an idea that comes up in 1618 and 1698 as well as in 1511.

As to the use of a major third around Pythagorean -- or possibly even
a full meantone diminished fourth -- in an ornamented cadence, Mark
Lindley suggests indications in some keyboard music around 1600 where
D# is consistently treated in this way as evidence for a regular
meantone in 12 notes (e.g. Eb-G#) with this liberty allowed. Again,
this is very much like Schlick's treatment of G# in his organ
compositions. This isn't to say it is an ideal, or even credible,
solution for every mid-17th century piece as played on a 12-note
instrument in a more or less regular meantone around 1/4-comma, only
that I'm not relying on Schlick in isolation to raise certain
possibilities that _might_ be tried for some pieces.

Also, I'd emphasize that some 16th-century as well as 17th-century
pieces clearly call for a proper D#; Vicentino uses it, as does Andrea
Gabrieli in a musical setting of the Oedipus drama where trying to
ornament every time it comes up would be quite untenable. Of course,
if the range happens to stay within Bb-D#, then on harpsichord one
could retune Eb to D#, and then retune it for pieces preferring the
more usual Eb. The Yale University organ, of course, would make such
retuning unnecessary, since it has both Eb and D#.

> To try out the new Yale University organ a few months ago (while it
> was still inside the factory in Virginia), I brought along a book
> of Froberger. The instrument has regular 1/4 comma everywhere, and
> several split keys for the subsemitones (two or three splits in
> each octave, except for the bass).

I'd be curious as to the precise accidentals. From your description, I
might guess something like Gb-A#, with 17 notes per octave.

> Well...it still didn't work. I tried out several pieces that were
> harmonically adventurous, from the 1640s-60s. And they just sounded
> stupid whenever notes such as E# and B# turned up, having no split
> key available. No amount of ornamentation salvaged such spots,
> especially in a piece by Froberger that *ends* each of its sections
> on a C# major triad!

My first reaction is that I would have advised them, if they're going
this far, to go just a bit further and have a standard 19-note tuning
of Gb-B#, which, for example, very nicely supports C#-E#-G#.

The main reason wouldn't necessarily be to play Froberger, whose music
Mark Lindley suggests is really best in 12-EDO, but to fit the style
of composers like Gesualdo and Trabaci who may use a range of Gb-B#,
and make a distinction, for example, between B# and C at a diesis
apart (I know specifically that Gesualdo does this).

This would be like the lower manual of Nicola Vicentino's arcicembalo
or (presumably) arciorgano, with 19 notes (also Gb-B#). There is a
theoretical consideration supporting this tuning size, standard on the
_cembalo cromatico_ around 1600 and also on some French instruments
like that of Costeley (which he started using in 1558, as I recall,
and described in 1570) at 1/3-comma or 19-EDO. We have what Erv Wilson
calls in modern terms a "Moment of Symmetry" (MOS) where there are
only two sizes of adjacent steps, here the meantone chromatic or minor
semitone at about 76 cents in 1/4-comma, and the enharmonic diesis at
128:125 or about 41 cents.

Here's a Scala file:

However, I certainly wouldn't be disappointed with that 17-note Gb-A#
instrument (which would be an MOS in a Pythagorean tuning, or some
modern regular temperament with fifths a bit larger than pure,
obviously both relevant to other styles than those we're discussing!).

There's a wealth of conventional and more adventurous 16th-century and
early 17th-century music that will fit beautifully on that new organ,
including, of course, pieces with both Eb and D#, or indeed chromatic
settings by Lasso that go up to A#.

While there might be a few pieces by Frescobaldi that might not fit,
there are lots and lots that will.

Also, where diminished fourths or augmented fifths are intended as
"special effects" intervals in meantone, they'll have their
characteristic proportions.

Having stated some advantages of 19 tones above, I should emphasize
that split-key instruments varied considerably in the number of notes
per octave, with 17 as one quite tenable compromise. The repertory is
large enough that the new Yale instrument should make lots of
beautiful music.

> Nor do I buy the fantasy argument that organists simply transposed
> the whole piece (if indeed they played composed pieces in public at
> all...); one of the Froberger toccatas I played needed not only
> E#/A#/D#/G#/C#/F#, but also Db/Ab/Eb/Bb and F and C. And it's
> definitely an organ piece: by its title, it's for use during the
> elevation of the host during a liturgy. There's no wholesale
> transposition of the composition available that would get it to
> work on extended regular 1/4 comma, even if all five raised keys
> got split...because it would also have to have one or more split
> *naturals*.

First of all, Lindley's suggestion of 12-EDO as Froberger's preferred
tuning would cover this kind of situation; it's an interesting
question whether, if Lindley's conclusion is correct, this might be
indirect evidence that Froberger was influenced in this taste by his
teacher Frescobaldi, maybe making Doni's story about the latter's
penchant for 12-EDO more credible. Then, again, the example of Zarlino
and his student Vincenzo Galilei cautions us that a teacher is not
necessarily responsible for all the views of her/his pupil -- although
Galilei, as it happens, expresses a liking for Zarlino's 2/7-comma as
the most pleasing keyboard temperament.

As I explained above, the "split naturals" as you call them of E#/F
and B#/C are standard in a 19-note meantone tuning of Gb-B#, which is
what was tuned in parts of France and Italy around 1600, and also
specifically commended by Praetorius in 1618 as solving lots of
accidental problems. With "an extended regular 1/4-comma" in this
size, no transposition would be needed, because all of your
accidentals specified above would be available in their usual places.

Just for fun, I would add that if your listing of accidentals for the
Froberger toccata is complete (Db-E#), one _could_ play this on a
17-note Gb-A# instrument by transposing down a fifth or up a fourth.
Then we would have Gb-A#, which just fits the range of the instrument.

This isn't to say that this fantasy scenario, as you term it, is an
ideal solution: if Froberger intended 12-EDO, I'd say that that is
what we should use, a fascinating early instance of its choice for an
organ piece.

However, if the folks at Yale want to try a transposition as an
interesting kind of variant reading, I'd say, "Go ahead, and let's
hear how it sounds." This is the same kind of thing that applies to
Aaron Johnson's meantone realizations of Elizabethan and Jacobean
keyboard music, where 1/3-comma or 19-EDO might be a bit heavier
temperament than Bull might have used, but is quite pleasing and
striking.

> The failure here is not the physical keyboard with its limitations,
> but rather the premise of 1/4 comma for such repertoire. I find it
> sad to see a brand-new, deluxe, expensive organ go into service,
> with a temperament scheme unable to play early-to-mid-17th-century
> core repertoire. (Some Frescobaldi would have problems, too.) But,
> they built it the way the customer wanted and ordered it....

Personally, I find it delightful to see a brand new organ go into
service with a temperament scheme ideally fitting a vast repertory of
16th-century and early 17th-century music -- much of it within a
conventional Eb-G# range, and some of it calling for 13-17 notes per
octave. Here I'm talking about the Renaissance and Manneristic eras,
say 1450-1650 or so. The fact that a few pieces might not fit, or
technically might be playable but really better adapted to other
tuning schemes, should hardly be cause for sadness, only an indication
that no one tuning scheme can cover everything.

Yes, 19 notes would have been yet more versatile than 17, and 31 notes
more versatile yet (a complete 1/4-comma circle) -- but the perfect
need not preclude the very good.

Also, I'm not offering 1/4-comma as a universal temperament solution.
The people at Yale might also want a Pythagorean organ for medieval
repertory (17 notes, Gb-A#, as described in the early 15th century,
would be ideal), and something in a irregular 12-note temperament of
some kind if they're going to play music after around 1650, or also a
few earlier pieces you've mentioned that might fit this kind of scheme.

Most appreciatively,

Margo Schulter
mschulter@calweb.com

11/23/2007 3:28:10 PM

Dear Brad,

Please let me quickly try to reply to your clarification about the
organ. I'd like to express my regrets for any needless controversy or
excitement in my remarks, especially when I have a responsibility to
set a different kind of example on this list, as some of your writings
very wisely remind me.

First, while trying to follow the information offered by the builder
of the Yale University organ still left me uncertain to exactly how
many subsemitones or split-key accidentals there are in an octave,
your estimate of 14 or 15 sounds very reasonable -- maybe G#/Ab,
Eb/D#, plus one other pair (C#/Db?).

If I now understand correctly, then obviously we're agreed that lots
of music likely intended for other kinds of tuning systems will be out
of its reach, as well as some 19-tone meantone repertory like the
_cembalo cromatico_ music around 1600 or some Gesualdo pieces.

However, this is hardly in my view a major fault of the Yale organ,
because I'd regard even a usual 12-note keyboard in 1/4-comma meantone
as incredibly versatile. In fact, I'd say that Eb/D# and G#/Ab should
handle a lot of commonly occurring situations around 1600.

If I were advising them, I would say that considering 19 notes per
octave would be good (Gb-A#). What I'd _really_ like to see is a
31-note instrument, which shows the full potential of 1/4-comma
and supports enharmonic or fifthtone pieces (e.g. Vicentino and
Colonna). Especially in a group which often celebrates explicitly
microtonal music, I consider this well worth mentioning.

Having said this, I'd like to focus on a few important points which
could be more important than technical niceties of transposition and the
like.

First, I'm really not in a good position to give opinions on what is
best for Froberger, Corelli, or Bach. This music is outside the
scope of what I'm familiar with and take as the main basis for my own
compositions and improvisations.

Having seen what happens when people discuss medieval and Renaissance
music, often in widely disseminated articles or textbooks, with what
seems to me less than comfortable familiarity with these styles, I
have no reason to suppose that I would do better with periods likewise
not so familiar to me. Further, I don't have the justification many of
these authors do that "the curriculum requires it."

Thus I should enact a self-denying ordinance excluding myself from the
"Bach's tuning" debate; people who are well-informed can get
themselves into enough dilemmas without my "help"!

Further, I would emphasize that a temperament like Zest-12 or Zest-24
is _not_ proposed as an "historical tuning" of any kind, but rather a
21st-century tuning that happens to fit certain stylistic directions.

Specifically, having mentioned some possible "compromises" in our
discussion, I would emphasize that I _don't_ consider ornamented
versions of standard 16th-17th century cadences as the best use for a
major third of 408 cents or 422 cents, say.

A far nobler use is to use such a third so that it will really be in
tune, whether as a meantone diminished fourth, or as a usual
neomedieval major third. Thus in Zest-12 or Zest-24, B-D# is much
nicer in some kind of neo-Gothic context (e.g. B-D#-G# before A#-E#-A#
or A-E-A) than in B-D#-F# before E-G#-B. Likewise, F#-A#-C# is
beautiful in a 13th-century style of cadence to G#-D#.

The sad thing about meantones, and especially some modified ones,
from a certain 21st-century perspective, is that you have all of these
wonderful wide major and small minor thirds that didn't really get
fully used in 16th-17th century period styles, because people weren't
aware of how nicely these intervals might fit more of a 13th-14th
century style.

Anyway, without taking any position on the Bach tuning question, I
must admire and commend your thoroughness in testing out different
pieces and seeking a solution "to taste" that can also fit the
historical evidence. That is a great contribution.

With many thanks,

Margo

Brad Lehman <bpl@umich.edu>

11/23/2007 4:12:12 PM

--- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, Margo Schulter <mschulter@...> wrote:
> First, while trying to follow the information offered by the builder
> of the Yale University organ still left me uncertain to exactly how
> many subsemitones or split-key accidentals there are in an octave,
> your estimate of 14 or 15 sounds very reasonable -- maybe G#/Ab,
> Eb/D#, plus one other pair (C#/Db?).

Take another look at the photo links I sent, especially this one:
http://www.taylorandboody.com/opuses/opus_55_pages/10_19_06/55keys.htm

1. Speaking first about the notes above tenor c:

All of the Eb/D# are split.

Most of the G#/Ab are split, but not all of them.

A few of the Bb/A# are split, but it's less than half of them on the
instrument.

No other splits. No Db, Gb, Cb, B#, or E# anywhere on the instrument.

If I remember correctly, from only about 20 minutes of playing it
some months ago, the Eb/D# and the G#/Ab play differently from each
other: the front half of the key plays the Eb or G# (i.e. the more
frequently-needed note), and the back half plays the D# or Ab. It
would have made more sense to me as a player to have the back half
always be the flat and the front half always be the sharp, or vice
versa, consistently across the whole keyboard.... The actual layout
makes it quite tricky to play things such as A-flat major triads,
using the front half of one split key with the back half of the other
one.

2. Now to the bass octave, everything below tenor c:

No subsemitones anywhere (not even for the G#/Ab); rather, all the
split keys there (but only available on one of the three manuals) are
to handle the short-octave layout.

That lowest octave is short, whether the keys are split or not: the
lever looking like "E" plays C, the F plays F, the "F#" plays D, the
G plays G, the "G#" plays E, the A plays A, and then the Bb and B do
what they look like.

The low F# and G# are available only on the lowest manual, the
Ruckpositif, by playing the back half of the split. On the other two
manuals those notes aren't available.

Lowest C# or Eb aren't available on any of the manuals. Compositions
requiring them *won't* be played.

3. And in the pedal it's fully chromatic, except that there's no
lowest C# or Db. Or any subsemitones (unless I'm mis-remembering the
pedalboard).

It seems to me, if Yale's folks wanted their organ students to learn
short-octave layouts and 1/4 comma meantone hands-on and intimately,
another good way to do so would be to have all their organ students
take at least a required year of harpsichord lessons, and give them
harpsichord tuning duties, on some suitable early-Italian or early-
German harpsichords. :)

Short-octave stuff is daily bread, with a couple of different
schemes, for some of the harpsichord repertoire by 17th century
Germans (and Froberger)...and especially so, going back into the 16th
in other countries as well.

I must also say, not in conjunction with Yale or anything else
specifically, but just to say it: the presence of split keys and/or
short octave on any keyboard instrument still doesn't constrain
anybody to use regular 1/4 on it, to the exclusion of other systems.
What's to stop anyone from tuning a subsemitones keyboard to regular
1/6 or anything else of one's fancy? (And the front half and the
back half would then be one Pyth comma apart....) The split keys
only provide an option to do some *different* note, and to have
either half be the higher-pitched half, to one's choosing. On an
organ, though, such things can't be changed day to day.

Brad Lehman

Ozan Yarman <ozanyarman@ozanyarman.com>

11/24/2007 5:00:37 PM

Dear Margo,

My apologies for the late reply. I have been unwell this week, that is why
my response is delayed.

I tried so long in vain to search for a temperament which could replace
53-equal, with two middle seconds (12:11 and 13:12 respectively) and with
strong 7-limit flavours. Other than 46-equal, I could locate only 34-equal
with a single 3/4 tone and minimal number of tones to work for maqam music.
I have aligned the frets of my bowed tanbur to 34 and appreciate very much
the results. Seeing as 34-equal is sort of an NI temperament, and is the
minimal tuning which would work for maqam music, I think it will do for a
new theory of maqamat.

Now, the issue is, whether we can have an acoustical piano which accomodates
34-equal. The black keys are easy enough if split in two for 17-equal. But I
wonder what can be done to switch between the bikechains of 17-equal on the
fly.

Cordially,
Oz.

----- Original Message -----
From: "Margo Schulter" <mschulter@calweb.com>

Subject: [tuning] Re: Two 31-equals spaced 15.42 cents apart so as to
achieve an NI interv

>
> > Inspired by Margo, I came up with a splendid maqam tuning with at least
one
> > nobly intoned ratio.
>
> > First, calculate the noble mediant between 27:25 and 14:13.
> > (27+14)*phi / (25+13)*phi=131.55 cents
>
> > In SCALA, type:
> > equal 31
> > copy 0 1
> > move
> > 131.55
> > merge
> > 1
> > Voila!
> > Oz.
>
> Dear Oz and Dave,
>
> Please let me diplomatically attempt here to mediate in a situation
> which I suspect must occur many times in the realm of musical practice
> and theory.
>
> Oz, you have used a mathematical formula which Dave devised and he and
> I documented for rather different purposes -- but to excellent effect,
> deriving a fine size of small neutral second for maqam music. The step
> of 131.55 cents should be especially welcome to Persian or Kurdish
> ears, for example, attuned to maqam or dastgah styles where a step on
> the order of 14:13 is very idiomatic. Here the mathematical noble
> mediant between 14:13 and 27:25 -- or also 1:1 and 13:12, as Dave has
> suggested -- happens to yield a fine melodic effect. I see no reason
> not to use this mathematical formula, or any mathematical formula,
> when it produces a result that fits the musical situation.
>
> Dave, you are equally correct that the concept of Noble Intonation
> was, if I am correct, conceived as especially applying to
> _simultaneous_ intervals which represent regions of maximum complexity
> between two simpler ratios. A classic illustration, of course, is the
> pair of major thirds at a just 5:4 and 9:7, with the NI region located
> somewhere around 422 cents.
>
> Thus I warmly agree that it would be a misunderstanding of the
> original NI concept to think that _any_ mathematical noble mediant
> between a pair of integer ratios will produce an audible "NI" effect.
> In fact, Oz's purpose seems to me quite different: to strike a kind of
> artful compromise between two sizes for _melodic_ steps, 14:13 and
> 27:25. The mathematical noble mediant operation happens to produce a
> most agreeable result -- although that wasn't its original purpose.
>
> Dave, I would say that one of the hallmarks of really great concepts
> and discoveries is that they may be used in various ways. Sometimes
> applications not anticipated by the discoverer may arise -- for better
> or worse -- and even be (mis)attributed to the discoverer.
>
> Here I would say, "Oz -- congratulations on a fine maqam tuning!"; and
> to Dave, "Indeed, while the formula was used here to pleasant effect,
> people should understand that the NI concept which gave rise to the
> formula is somewhat different."
>
> With warmest wishes to you both, and all,
>
> Margo Schulter
> mschulter@calweb.com
>
>

> Take another look at the photo links I sent, especially this one:

Thank you for this specific link. One lesson I've learned from this is
that especially with a site where there isn't such easy navigation
with a text-based browser, I would do better to download and save your
post in HTML format and then follow the URL's with links rather than
lynx (the latter of which is more compatible with Yahoo).

> If I remember correctly, from only about 20 minutes of playing it
> some months ago, the Eb/D# and the G#/Ab play differently from each
> other: the front half of the key plays the Eb or G# (i.e. the more
> frequently-needed note), and the back half plays the D# or Ab. It
> would have made more sense to me as a player to have the back half
> always be the flat and the front half always be the sharp, or vice
> versa, consistently across the whole keyboard.... The actual layout
> makes it quite tricky to play things such as A-flat major triads,
> using the front half of one split key with the back half of the
> other one.

What I suspect, Brad, is that this question of preference might be one
indication of what repertories a given player is most familiar with or
gives highest priority. You are, of course, correct that Ab-C-Eb or
the like would be easier if flats were aligned with flats, and sharps
with sharps, consistently.

My own preference would be for the rather standard 16th-century
approach they've evidently taken, which is also Vicentino's, for
example. I would regard Eb and G# as "usual," and D# or Ab as more
"unusual."

While I haven't experienced this issue directly with split keys
proper, I have dealt with the analogous situation of mapping a 17-note
circulating system to a potentially 24-note keyboard with two standard
12-note manuals. Do I put all the flats on one keyboard and the sharps
on another, or do Eb-G# on the lower keyboard and the more "unusual"
accidentals on the other?

I've tried both, and preferred the Eb-G# arrangement as more familiar,
although it is much _less_ convenient for Ab-Eb, especially if this
occurs with an octave, Ab-Eb-Ab -- and likewise with G#-D#. Well, with
a 17-note circle, at least there's the compensation of having the
fifth A#-Gb (or A#-E# for an equivalent spelling) with both notes on
the same keyboard.

Actually, with two 12-note manuals, I find that I tend to go for
24-note tunings where the two manuals have identical steps and
intervals at some fixed distance apart (e.g. 1/4-comma or 2/7-comma
meantone at the enharmonic diesis, or Zest-24, a kind of modified
variation on the latter, with 18 or the 24 notes identical to a
regular 24-note tuning).

> I must also say, not in conjunction with Yale or anything else
> specifically, but just to say it: the presence of split keys and/or
> short octave on any keyboard instrument still doesn't constrain
> anybody to use regular 1/4 on it, to the exclusion of other
> systems. What's to stop anyone from tuning a subsemitones keyboard
> to regular 1/6 or anything else of one's fancy? (And the front half
> and the back half would then be one Pyth comma apart....) The split
> keys only provide an option to do some *different* note, and to
> have either half be the higher-pitched half, to one's choosing. On
> an organ, though, such things can't be changed day to day.

Please let me enthusiastically second this very important point, which
indeed might easily get overlooked among the specifics of a discussion
about the Yale organ. Interestingly, one historical tuning that very
much comes to mind shares with your 1/6-comma example a set of split
accidentals at Pythagorean comma apart: a 17-note Pythagorean tuning
(Gb-A#) of the kind advocated by Prosdocimus de Beldemando and Ugolino
or Orvieto in the earlier 15th century.

A keyboard supporting this 15th-century tuning scheme -- Lindley isn't
sure if it might have been implemented at that time -- would also
nicely support a modern 17-note circle, equal or unequal (as with
George Secor's 17-WT). Or, of course, it could support 17 notes of any
desired meantone temperament.

Again, I've had experience only with a crude equivalent of real split
keys: tuning certain keys on two 12-note MIDI keyboards to different
notes, e.g. C# on the lower keyboard and Db on the upper, or F and E#,
etc. I'd suspect that even with the Ab-C-Eb complication you describe
(if my curious preference for Eb and G# at the front is followed),
actual split keys might be a bit easier than having to find the notes
for such a sonority on two different manuals -- a good
finger-stretching exercise, as I've reminded myself trying it a moment
ago.

Best,

Margo

Dear Ozan,

Please let me thank you for your mention of 17-EDO and 34-EDO. This
reminds me of a 34-note system I proposed some years ago, and which
looks very interesting for maqam music -- as long as you can do
without ratios of 5.

The system uses two complete circles or "bicycle chains" of 17-EDO at
about 55.106 cents apart.

! 17x2_55.scl
!
Two complete 17-tET chains spaced for pure 7:6 (~55.106 cents)

1129.41176

Note that there quite reasonable approximations of septimal flavors
(including some pure 7:6 thirds), and also of 14:13 and 13:12. The
large neutral second at around 156 cents is rather larger than 12:11;
but there are nice approximations of 11:8 and 16:11, with the 55-cent
interval between the two chains rather close to 33:32.

For Persian or Kurdish music, the 126-cent step should be quite
pleasing, and also the small 337-cent neutral third (near 243:200),
which is not far from the NI size of around 339 cents. Of course, as
you note, the 424-cent major third of 17-EDO is very close to NI.

An interesting touch is that we get a minor third size very close to
19:16, formed by the larger neutral second steps of 141 and 156
cents. This is quite close to the 64:59:54 division mentioned in some
medieval Islamic theory.

My guess would be that the variety of neutral intervals might be
attractive for maqam music, if the idea is to have a comparatively
small system where each note has its perfect fifth and fourth.

With many thanks,

Margo

Dear Margo,

divide alike Zarlino once had done, the

SC = 81/80 = (81*7)/(80*7) = 567/560

into 4 arithmetic subparts [2/7 + 2/7 + 2/7] + 1/7 in his manner:

567/560 = [ (567/565) * (565/563) * (563/561) ] * (561/560) = 81/80

That yields in Werckmeister's terms of 8 just pure 5ths
an corresponding 4-fold subdivision of the:

PC = 3^12/2^19 = (81/80)*(32768/32805) = SC * schisma

into

C 565/567 G 563/565 D 561/563 A-E-B (560/561)(32768/32805)
F#-C#-G#-D#-A#-Bb-F-C

or 'septenarian' circulating:

begin=7=Ab-Eb-Bb-F-C~G~D~A-E-B~F#-G#=7=end

expanded multiplying consecutive the following lines by factor 3

Ab_-2: 7 cps or Hz
Eb_1: 21 (> 20.777777777...)
Bb_2: 63 (> 62.3333333333...)
F_3: 189 (> 187 = 561/3)
C_5: 567 (> 565 (> 563 (> 561 = 187*3 )))
temper down by: (1 200 * ln(565 / 567)) / ln(2) = ~-6.11744117...Cents
G_6: 1695 (> 1689 (> 1683 = 187*9 ))
temper down by: (1 200 * ln(563 / 565)) / ln(2) = ~-6.13913427...Cents
D_8: 5067 (> 5049 = 187*27)
temper down by: (1 200 * ln(561 / 563)) / ln(2) = ~-6.16098177...Cents
A_10: 15147 = 187*81
E_12: 45441 = 187*243
B_13: 136323 = 187*729...(> B-7: 7/27)
lower (1200*ln((560/561)*(32768/32805)))/ln(2) = ~-5.04245318...Cents
(F#_15: 187*2187>) F#_-5: 7/9
C#_-3: 7/3
G#_-2: 7 cycle closed enharmonic back to start: Ab_-2: 7 cps or Hz

That's chromatic in ascending pitch order as absolute frequencies
when taken modulo 2^n into the middle octave:

c 283.5 = 567/2 "middle_C"
# 298.6666666... = 298+2/3 = 128*7/3
d 316.6875 = 5067/16
# 336 = 21*16
e 355.007812 = 45441/128
f 378 = 189*2
# 398.2222222... = 398+2/9 = 512*7/9
g 423.75 = 1695/4
# 448 = 7*64
a 473.34375 = 473+11/32 = 15147/32 ~Praetorius high Choir-Thone~
# 504 = 63*8
b 532.511719.. = 532+131/256 = 17*11*3^7/2^8
c'567 "tenor_C"

for the corresponding lower Cammerthone version
simply divide each pitch by 9/8 by of an major-tone downwards.

so that:

c_4 becomes 252 Hz = (567*4/9)cps and
a_4 = 1683/4 = 420.75 Hz

in order to replace the my meanwhile outdated 9.9.99
first original "squiggle" 420Hz proposal:
http://www.strukturbildung.de/Andreas.Sparschuh/
by the above new improved version, the now actual:

Rational 2/7-SC-"squiggle" interpretation absolute @ a'=420.75cps

that tempers barely 4 of the dozen 5ths
just in Werckmeister's famous 8-pure 5ths layout,
instead fromerly only 4 pure 5ths once in 1999 at
DA&F#C#G#Eb. Meanwhile, now that turns out in my
in my ears as suspicious to much near
inbetween Kellners modern PC^(1/5) schmeme
or even worser others ahistoric alleged PC^(1/6) claims.

Attend that:
Above Zarlino's arithmetic 2/7-SC division should not be
confused with its modern irrational approximation:

(81/80)^(2/7) = ~1.0035556...
(1 200 * ln((81 / 80)^(2 / 7))) / ln(2) = ~6.14465417...Cents

Margo Schulter <mschulter@calweb.com>

12/5/2007 1:00:45 PM

Dear Daniel (and All),

Thank you for your very thoughtful remarks about "microtonality" as
one dimension of musical technique or choice rather than a discrete
genre. I very much agree, and might offer an illustration involving
modal music.

Please let me say that I do this with great modesty, given that my
range of experience both in composition and theory may be quite
circumscribed compared to yours in many areas. Given that your focus
is mainly tonal, and mine modal, it's interesting to ask how this
might influence intonational preferences. Quite possibly, we might
draw sometimes on similar interval sizes used in somewhat different
contexts, just as two natural languages might have some similar
phonemes used in quite distinct grammatical systems.

Given your mention of meantone, I thought I might again post an mp3
file and PDF score for a piece, _Intrada in F Lydian_, which I linked
to in an article of some weeks ago.

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/IntradaFLydian.mp3>
<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/IntradaFLydian.pdf>

If I were asked to describe the genre of this piece, I might say
"Manneristic," referring to the era of around 1540-1640 in European
music. More specifically the piece uses a variation on Zarlino's
2/7-comma meantone; but a listener might hear and recognize the
general period style without focusing on this intonational aspect,
much less the fact that this is a _modified_ meantone where the major
third and sixth in Eb-G-C, for example, will be a full syntonic comma
wider than in Zarlino's regular temperament.

While the intonational dimension is indeed meant to be significant, I
suspect that many other aspects of counterpoint and harmony around
1600 play a central role in shaping the piece. It would be quite
possible to use a meantone (regular or modified) for some tonal music
around 1720, say; but here the style would produce different results,
even were the interval sizes quite similar.

A metaphor I am tempted to draw involves the early years of
typesetting and page layout on personal computers, then widely
celebrated as "desktop publishing." A common refrain of experienced
typesetters in that era, and even of some not-so-experienced ones, was
that the main emphasis needed to be placed on desktop _publishing_.
In others, simply learning a given software program was not a
satisfactory substitute for the often more daunting task of mastering
the craft of typography and graphic design.

Similarly, understanding or emulating the style of Nicola Vicentino
involves more than understanding meantone tunings, enharmonic dieses
or fifthtones in a temperament around 1/4-comma or 31-EDO, and the
altered intervals which occur in a 31-note circle. The style is also
shaped by the conventional patterns of 16th-century counterpoint and
harmony to which Vicentino devotes much of his treatise of 1555.

Please let me say that although I recognize the merits of your
observation that sometimes sticking to theoretical discussions avoids
the possible sensitivities and even injuries that might arise when a
given piece by a given participant becomes a focus for discussion, yet
I would love to hear some of your music, whether posted here or
available elsewhere. I am sure that it offers much enjoyment and
education alike.

Most appreciatively,

Margo Schulter
mschulter@calweb.com

Magnus Jonsson <magnus@smartelectronix.com>

12/7/2007 3:46:52 PM

Dear Magnus,

Thank you warmly for your enthusiastic response to _Intrada in F
Lydian_, and also your Web site, which includes some evidently very
extended pieces that I look forward to hearing.

An essay by Siemen Terpstra which you have very helpfully made
available on your site raises some issues about the potentials of
modified meantone tunings, and how the resources of these tunings may
resemble or different somewhat from those of 31-EDO or a very similar
31-note circle of 1/4-comma meantone.

Currently I am exploring some of these questions in a composition
using Zest-24, a tuning based on two 12-note circles of the modified
meantone system used for the Intrada. Your generous encouragement,
your own music and ideas, and the Terpstra essay you have made
available all lend impetus to this effort.

I am not sure if I will be able to complete this composition before
leaving for a holiday visit with my mother, but whenever I have an
opportunity to do so and share the piece with you, please take it as a
tribute to your music and wise counsel.

Most appreciatively,

Margo Schulter
mschulter@calweb.com

Margo Schulter <mschulter@calweb.com>

12/7/2007 3:47:57 PM

Dear Daniel,

Please let me add my tribute to Stockhausen and his music. I recall
hearing it in an undergraduate course in contemporary music --
curiously, the one college course in music that I can recall taking.
His music indeed sounded very distinctive, modern, and awesome.

Of course, I regard intonation as only one dimension of musicality,
and by no means always the most significant in shaping a composer's or
listener's sense of identity and affinity. Thus I would regard the
neomodality of Debussy or Vaughan Williams as a shaping influence of
my own music, for example, as well as the modality and quartal/quintal
styles of Bartok, quite regardless of whether this music happens to be
performed in 12-EDO or some other system.

Thus I express my great affection and appreciation to Stockhausen for
having conceived of such unique and transforming edifices of sound --
rather as I am enthralled by aural architectonics of Perotin. Of
course, as Stockhausen's own use of intonational variation shows, a
plurality of tuning systems can multiply the possibilities.

Most appreciatively,

Margo Schulter
mschulter@calweb.com

12/17/2007 12:04:45 AM

Hello, everyone.

Since I'm leaving tomorrow for a visit with my Mom, please let me say that
I'm in the middle of a composition for Annika (also known as the daughter
of the one and only AKJ) that I hope to finish when I get back next month.

Also, why don't I share some notes about a subset of Zest-24 that I'm
exploring called Goya/Supergoya. The basic Goya-17 features a set of
major, minor, and neutral intervals with reference to a "1/1" of Db that
somewhat resemble, but often radically differ from, a 17-tone circle such
as George Secor's 17-WT. Generally major septimal intervals from Db are
approximated using the chain of fifths on the lower keyboard, while the
upper keyboard supplies approximations of minor septimal intervals and
also neutral intervals, as explained more in the notes.

Supergoya-17plus3 adds some approximations of septimal ratios such as
21:16 and 189:128. This makes some basic 17-tone-style resolutions
available at more locations, but also facilitates idioms which favor
sonorities such as 16:21:24:28 or the narrow and wide fourths and fifths
(and octaves!) of gamelan.

Here's a link to this very rough sketch, more of an essay in progress
than anything, but possibly curious to some here.

<http://www.bestII.com/~mschulter/letter-goya-supergoya.txt>

Also, here's a Scala file of a tuning set which I hope by some time early
next year will have some mp3 files to illustrate its potentials -- from
me, with others warmly encouraged to join in also if this system looks
interesting.

! zest24-supergoya17plus3_Db.scl
!
Goya-17 plus 484, 676, and 1180 cents
20
!

it is also possible to read Werckmeister's #3 pattern

C~G~D~A E B~F#...C

in 1/3 SC terms:

C 242/243 G 241/242 D 240/241 A E B 32768/32805 F# C# G# D# Bb F C

as refinement of his JI tuning presented in his book:
"Musicae mathematicae hodegus curiosus"
FFM 1687: p.71: a'=400cps
extracted from his "Natï¿½rlich" (natural) scale,
there defined in absolute pitch-frequencies:

c" 480 cps
(db 512)
c# 500
d" 540
d# 562.5
eb 576
e" 600
f" 640
f# 675
g" 720
g# 750
ab 768
a" 800 overtaken from Mersenne's reference-tone a'=400Hz
b" 864
h" 900
c"'960

The W3 pattern can be understood as
modification of layout pattern,
in absolute terms,
as cycle of partially tempered 5hts:

Db 1 unison, implicit contained in his absolute "hodegus" tuning
Ab 3
Eb 9
Bb 27
F 81 (>80+2/3 (>80+1/3 (80 40 20 10 5)))
C 243 (>242 (>241 (>240 120 60 30 15)))
G (729 >) 726 (>723 (>720 360 180 90 45))
D 2169 (>2160 1080 540 270 135)
A 405 compare to Chr. Hygens(1629-95) Amsterdam determination:~407 Hz
E 1215
B 3645
F# (10935=32805/3 >) 32768/3 ... 1/3
C# 1 returend back unison again

that's relative in chromatically ascending order as Scala-file:

!Werckmeister3_one3rd_SC_variant.scl
!
Werckmeister's famous C~G~D-A-E-B~F#...C pattern as 1/3 SC + schisma
!C 242/243 G 241/242 D 240/241 A E B 32768/32805 F# C#=Db Ab Eb Bb F C
!
256/243 ! Db=C# enharmonics @ absolute Mersenne's 256cps unison
241/216 ! D
32/27 ! Eb
5/4 ! E
4/3 ! F
1024/729 ! F#
121/81 ! G = (11/9)^2 = (3/2)*(243/242)
128/81 ! Ab
5/3 ! A
16/9 ! Bb
15/8 ! B (german H)
2/1

attend:
That one contains more pure intervals than other interpretations.
